EXECUTIVE SUMMARY 

Location of the Property 

The area covered by the Makola permit is located about 500km west of Brazzaville, Republic of 
Congo (ROC), and directly northeast of the port city Point Noire. The Makola permit area lies in 
the Kouilou province, its boundaries are given by the following coordinates (official datum in 
ROC Pointe Noire 1948) in the decree 2009-204: 

Corner Points Longitude Latitude 

A 11° 51' 36" E 4° 31' 43" S 

B 12° 01' 45" E 4° 22' 35" S 

C 12° 16' 24" E 4° 40' 25" S 

D 11° 58' 57" E 4° 56' 29" S 

E 11° 55' 40" E 4° 45' 00" S 

F 12° 01' 03" E 4° 45' 00" S 

G 12° 01' 03" E 4° 37' 37" S 

H 11° 55' 40" E 4° 37' 37" S 

The area comprises 1,111km² and excludes MAGMINERALS Exploitation License area near the 
village of Mengo approximately 20km north east of the city of Pointe Noire.  

Land Tenure 

The authors have not completed a detailed review of the mineral titles or agreements to assess 
the validity of the stated ownership of the Makola Exploration Permit and relies on the docu-
mentation provided by MAGMINERALS. The Makola Exploration Licence was granted for Po-
tassium salts and related salts by the Decree 2009-204 dated 20 July 2009, in accordance with 
law 23/82 dated 7 July 1982, enacting the Mining Code.  

The Makola Exploration License gives the holder exclusive right to carry out exploration activi-
ties in the area defined, to an unlimited depth and in respect to potassium salts. The Makola 
Exploration Licence is valid is valid for 3 years and can be extended twice for a period of 2 
years each time upon application by the holder. The permit can be withdrawn by the Minister in 
charge of mines if the holder does not start exploration activities for the mineral substance it 
was issued for within 9 months of the date at which the permit was granted. Depending on the 
results of the exploration activities carried out under the exploration permit, the permit holder 
shall have priority as regards the granting of an exploitation permit for the mineral substances 
covered by the relevant exploration permit. 

Geology and Mineralisation 

Geologically, the deposit area is located in the Congo coastal basin of Aptian age (lower Cre-
taceous), which extends from Equatorial Guinea in the North, to Angola in the South (FEUGA ET 
AL., 2005, /12/). The basin represents the eastern flank of the pre-Atlantic continental rift basin 
which continues on the opposite coast of the South Atlantic Ocean in the Sergipe Basin, Brazil, 
where large potash deposits are still under extraction. To the east this basin is bordered by 
Precambrian crystalline rocks, known as the Mayombe basement.

For the whole Congolese coastal basin non NI 43-101 compliant geological resources have 
been estimated at 800 billion tonnes of carnallitite by the Congolese government. For the 
1300km² Holle Mine Area FEUGA ET AL. (2005) /12/ cite from COMPAGNIE DE POTASSE DE 
CONGO (CPC) sources non NI 43-101 compliant geological K2O resources of 30 billion tonnes 
of Sylvite1 and Carnallite. The detail of the data available for both these historical resource es-
timates is below that required for inferred resources according to CIM Standards (CIM 2005 
/1/). MAGMINERALS is not treating these historical estimates as current mineral resources as 
defined in NI 43-101. They are presented here because they are considered relevant and of 
historic significance. These historic estimates should not be relied upon, but suggest that a 
potash deposit in the form of Carnallite underlies the Makola Exploration License area.  

Exploration 

The potash exploration and mining history of the Makola License area is given below  

Years Company Activity 

pre- 1961 varied  about 15 drill holes and 1,000km of 2D reflection seismic for oil 
exploration over the Congo coastal basin 

1960 - 1970 SPC about 71 drill holes for combined potash and oil exploration (~ 
46 mainly concentrated at the later Holle mine)   

1969 - 1977 CPC operation of the Holle Sylvie mine with a total production of 
about 4 million tonnes of K2O over this time 

1974 - 1987 TOTAL/ ELF  about 15 drill holes and 1,500 km of 2D reflection seismic for oil 
exploration in the southern part of the Congo coastal basin.  

1987 - 1991 MDPA 4 drill holes for potash exploration and 10 drill holes, 10 km of 
high definition reflection seismic and several other geophysical 
methods to define the anomalous zone at the former Holle mine. 

1998 MAGALLOY 1 drill hole Mengo 1 for Carnallite exploration 

2005-2007 MAGMINERALS 12 drill holes and 23 km of 2D seismic survey for carnallitite ex-
ploration 

No further potash exploration activities are known to have occurred in the license area. From 
the historical exploration drilling about 95 drill holes with data about potash mineralisation were 
available. Between end of 2008 and beginning of 2010 MagMinerals has drilled 25 production 
holes in a small area of the Mengo Exploitation Licence. For this study no additional exploration 
activities have been conducted. 

Deposit and Mineralisation 

The Congo coastal Basin contains 2 potash bearing minerals;  

• Sylvite, chemical formula KCl  

• Carnallite, chemical formula KMgCl3 x 6 H2O 

A rock consisting mainly of Sylvite together with Halite is called sylvinite. Subordinately anhy-
drite, clay and or dolomite can occur. The latter two are taken together as insoluble material in 
the assays.  

A rock consisting mainly of Carnallite, together with Halite is called carnallitite. Subordinately 
the highly soluble salt minerals Tachyhydrite and/or Bischofite as well as anhydrite, clay and or 
dolomite can occur.  

                                                
1 Sylvite, KCl, Carnallite KMgCl3 x 6H2O

O:\@Projekte EGB aktuell\EGB_10-012_MagIndustries_Makola_DeskStudy\03proj_AUSLIEFERUNG\NI43-101\Final_Report\2010_08_MagIndustries_Makola-rev-01.docx 

In general sylvinite deposits consist of relatively thin beds, with KCl content between 20 and 
35%, whereas carnallitite deposits usually consist of relatively thick beds with KCl content be-
tween 8 and 24%.  

The sylvinite in the Congo coastal basin is usually considered secondary and requires that the 
original carnallitite deposit type has reacted with water according to the reaction:  

Carnallite + Water = Sylvite + MgCl2 rich brine. 

The presence of water in the carnallitite salt rocks is a consequence of fault zones or other dis-
turbances present. The sylvinite deposits in the Congo coastal basin, therefore, occur only lo-
cally and are mostly structurally controlled. In the Makola Exploration License sylvinite occurs 
along the Holle mine structure, where it was conventionally mined, till the mine flooded in 1977 
(FEUGA ET AL., 2005 /12/). 

The carnallitite in the Congo Coastal Basin, which is the dominant deposit type, is considered 
primary. Within the Congo coastal basin numerous carnallitite layers occur within the evaporite 
sequence. Single carnallitite horizons can be traced over up to 30km. Therefore the geological 
model for the deposit considers that the carnallitite occurs in subhorizontal layers, extending 
over large areas, which are only locally disturbed.  

Carnallitite layers with thickness and grade suitable for mining (Thickness > 4m, Grade > 6% 
K2O), that can be correlated over most of the Makola License area are called horizons, whereas 
layers that only have been identified in single drill holes or in a small part of the license are are 
called layers.  

Resource and Reserve Estimation 

In total 16 different potash bearing layers have been identified within the Makola License area, 
that have economic potential to be mined either by solution mining and/or conventional mining. 
In total assaying data were available for 133 potash layers of horizons and the following is the 
averaged thickness and K2O content of the different mineralized layers/horizons estimated from 
these data are in descending order:  

• Horizon 1 carnallitite: 4 holes with 19.2m thickness at 10.5% K2O 

• Horizon 2 carnallitite: 6 holes with 8.4m thickness at 9.1% K2O 

• Horizon 2 sylvinite: 2 holes with 3.3m thickness at 15.7% K2O 

• Layer 9 of cycle IX carnallitite: 1 hole with 4.4m thickness at 11.8% K2O 

• Layers 6,7 and 8 of cycle IX carnallitite:8 holes with 7.9m thickness at 8.6% K2O 

• Layers 6,7 and 8 of cycle IX sylvinite:1 hole with 7.4m thickness at 13.6% K2O 

• Horizon 3 carnallitite: 28 holes with 8.7m thickness at 15.8% K2O 

• Horizon 3 sylvinite: 9 holes with 3.0m thickness at 39.1% K2O 

• Layer 4 of cycle VIII carnallitite: 1 hole with 5.8m thickness at 11.2% K2O 

• Horizon 4 carnallitite: 34 holes with 11.6m thickness at 12.6% K2O 

• Horizon 4 sylvinite: 2 holes with 3.1m thickness at 35.5% K2O 

• Layer 1 of cycle VIII carnallitite: 2 holes with 4.8m thickness at 14.3% K2O 

• Horizon 5 carnallitite: 8 holes with 4.5m thickness at 9.2% K2O 

• Layers 3 and 4 of cycle VII carnallitite:7 holes with 6.0m thickness at 9.6% K2O

The resources have been categorized based on the distance surrounding the historical drill 
holes. This is the best available practice in the potash industry. The distances from the wells for 
inclusion in the inferred resources are as follows:  

3000m radius for carnallitite Horizons that could be correlated over most of the licence 
areas  

1500m radius for sylvinite Horizons or carnallitite/sylvinite layers that could not be corre-
lated between several drill holes, or were only identified in restricted areas of the licence 
area. 

On the basis of the diameters discussed above, polygons were constructed around the drill 
holes. Based on a coarse geologic model the location of a number of fault zones have been 
identified, which might pose problems during mining of the potash ore and, therefore, these 
areas have been excluded from the resource area. Also the underground mine workings of the 
flooded Holle mine have been excluded from the resource area. For the inferred resources in 
carnallitite horizons and layers an overall 35% area reduction for geological and technical ex-
clusion areas has been used. For sylvinite horizons or layers an overall 50% area reduction for 
geological and technical exclusion areas has been used. Not included in the estimate are min-
ing losses, dilution and plant recovery.  

The estimated inferred resources for the different horizons/layers are summarized in Table 1 for 
sylvinite and in Table 2 for carnallitite.

Conclusions 

It is the opinion of the authors that within the Makola License area inferred resources of about 
9200 million tonnes of carnallitite ore at an average grade of 12% K2O and 70 million tonnes of 
sylvinite ore at an average grade of 21% K2O occur.  

This is a conservative estimate of the inferred resources as (i) only about 25% of the license 
area is covered by confidence areas around drill holes and (ii) in the available drill holes not all 
potash horizons/layers drilled through have been assayed.  

In the opinion of the authors the Makola Exploration License area contains sufficient inferred 
resources to justify further expenditures of funds to confirm and expand the resource and to 
develop indicated and measured resources and reserves in compliance with NI 43-101. Explo-
ration, engineering, geotechnical, and economic studies are required to develop indicated and 
measured resources and to estimate reserves.  

Recommendations 

A four phases development for the project is proposed consisting of: 

1. Confirmation Phase, is estimated to cost about 2.3 million EURO and could be com-
pleted in 10 months. This phase confirms the historical data and provides information

4. Feasibility Phase, costs and time frame cannot be reasonably estimated.

1 Introduction 

MAGMINERALS POTASH CORP. ("MAGMINERALS") requested ERCOSPLAN Ingenieurgesell-
schaft Geotechnik und Bergbau mbH ("ERCOSPLAN") to evaluate the potential of the Makola 
Exploration Licence area, Kouilou Region, Republic of Congo ("the Property") for potash re-
sources on basis of available historical data in the Makola area. Dr Henry A M Rauche, Euro-
pean Geologist, and Dr Sebastiaan N G C van der Klauw, European Geologist, both Senior 
Geologists (for ERCOSPLAN) served as the Qualified Persons responsible for the preparation 
of this Technical Report as defined in National Instrument 43-101 and in compliance with Form 
43-101F1. 

The scope of work entailed review of pertinent geological, geophysical and other data in suffi-
cient detail to prepare a mineral resource estimate as well as a Technical Report to extends 
MAGMINERALS resource base.  

The historical data available for the study come mainly from the former archive of MINES DE 
POTASSES DE ALSACE (MDPA) that was involved in several phases of exploration activity in 
ROC and in exploitation of the Holle mine. The data available for the Makola License area con-
sist of  

• Drill hole data of over 80 exploration drill holes and 26 production drill holes 
varying between complete drill reports with assays and geophysical logging to a 
drill log interpreted from geophysical data that are not available.  

• 2D Seismic data, consisting of low resolution paper prints, 

• yearly mine plans of the Holle sylvinite mine 

• miscellaneous documentation about test work (e.g. rock mechanics, flotation 
tests)   

Besides the historical data from the archive, also the results of the MAGMINERALS potash ex-
ploration campaign 2004-2007 near the village of Mengo were available (ERCOSPLAN 2009 
/11/). 

Dr Henry Rauche personally inspected the property from 16 to 19 September 2007. During this 
inspection the recent Mengo drill sites, as well as the flooded Holle mine were visited. Dr Se-
bastiaan van der Klauw personally inspected the property and supervised the exploration pro-
gram for the Mengo property with several visits between 2005 and 2009 (27.08. - 31.08.2005, 
04.11. - 14.11.2005, 04.07. - 02.08.2006, 09.08. - 11.09.2006, 18.09. - 09.10.2006, 30.10. - 
17.11.2006, 29.12. - 17.01.2007, 27.02.2007 – 14.03.2007, 18.04 – 04.05.2007, 05.05 – 
07.05.2008, 06.12.2008 – 05.01.2009, 04.05 – 20.05.2009).

3 Property Description and Location 

The area covered by the Makola permit is located about 500km west of Brazzaville, Republic of 
Congo (ROC), and directly northeast of the port city Point Noire. The Makola permit area lies in 
the Kouilou province, its boundaries are given by the following coordinates (official datum in 
ROC Pointe Noire 1948) in the decree 2009-204: 

 

Corner Points Longitude Latitude 

A 11° 51' 36" E 4° 31' 43" S 

B 12° 01' 45" E 4° 22' 35" S 

C 12° 16' 24" E 4° 40' 25" S 

D 11° 58' 57" E 4° 56' 29" S 

E 11° 55' 40" E 4° 45' 00" S 

F 12° 01' 03" E 4° 45' 00" S 

G 12° 01' 03" E 4° 37' 37" S 

H 11° 55' 40" E 4° 37' 37" S 

 

The area comprises 1,111km² (Figure 1) and excludes MAGMINERALS Exploitation License 
area near the village of Mengo approximately 20km north east of the city of Pointe Noire.  

3.1 Land Tenure and Property Title in Republic of Congo 

ERCOSPLAN has not completed a detailed review of the mineral titles or agreements to assess 
the validity of the stated ownership of the Makola Exploration Permit and relies on the docu-
mentation provided by MAGMINERALS. Appendix  1 shows the Makola Exploration Licence, 
which was granted for Potassium salts and related salts by the Decree 2009-204 of 20 July 
2009, to SOCIETÈ POTASSES DU CONGO S.A.R.L., which was acquired by MAGMINERALS in 
October 20092.   

With the Makola Exploration Licence MAGMINERALS has a priority option to obtain an exploita-
tion permit within the exploration area. 

The mining policy of the RoC is based upon the constitution of the RoC dated 20 January 2002, 
the law 4/2005 of 11 April 2005 enacting the mining code, the law 23/82 of 7 July 1982 and 
decree 86/814 dated 11 June 1986 implementing the mining code. These laws were estab-
lished to stimulate the development of mining and to guarantee the property rights of both local 
and foreign investors. According to the law, the state owns all mineral resources, but explora-
tion and exploitation of these resources by private parties is permitted through different types of 
mining permits, which are granted by decrees. The permit holders have both rights and obliga-
tions, as defined by the Mining Code enacted in the law 4/2005 of 11 April 2005.

but will be added as Appendix as soon as available

Figure 1 Sketch Map for the Location of the MagIndustries Properties in the ROC 

Mining rights in the RoC are obtained in two or three stages: 

(1) The authorisation to prospect ("L'autorisation de prospection") allows geological 
prospecting at the earth surface for a defined mineral substance. This authorisa-
tion is non exclusive and can be simultaneously granted to several parties. The 
authorisation is valid for 1 year and can be extended for another year. The au-
thorisation can be extended to include other mineral substances upon applica-
tion by the holder of such authorisation. Depending on the results of the pros-
pecting activities the holder of such  authorisation may apply for: 

(i) a permit for mineral exploration 

(ii) an authorisation for exploitation 

for the minerals and area covered by the authoristion to prospect.  

(2) The Exploration Permit ("Permis de recherches minières") gives the holder ex-
clusive prospecting and exploration rights in a defined area to unlimited depth 
for the mineral substances it is issued for. According to law 4/2005 of 11 April 
2005 it is valid for 3 years and can be extended twice for a period of 2 years 



 
 
 
 
Technical  Scoping  Study, Makola Exploration License, Kouilou Region, Republic of Congo 
EGB 10-012 
 
 

 
Page 20 of 97 

 

ERCOSPLAN Ingenieurgesellschaft Geotechnik und Bergbau mbH 
Arnstaedter Strasse 28, 99096 Erfurt, Germany 

 

Phone: + 49 361 3810 220  Fax: +49 361 3810 402 e-mail: mining@ercosplan.com web: http://www.ercosplan.com 
 

O:\@Projekte EGB aktuell\EGB_10-012_MagIndustries_Makola_DeskStudy\03proj_AUSLIEFERUNG\NI43-101\Final_Report\2010_08_MagIndustries_Makola-rev-01.docx 

each time upon application by the holder. The permit can be withdrawn by the 
Minister in charge of mines if the holder does not start exploration activities for 
the mineral substance it was issued for within 9 months of the date at which the 
permit was granted. Depending on the results of the exploration activities carried 
out under the exploration permit, the permit holder shall have priority as regards 
the granting of an exploitation permit for the mineral substances covered by the 
relevant exploration permit.    

(3) The Exploitation Permit ("Permis d'exploitation") gives the holder exclusive right 
to carry out exploitation activities in an area defined by the permit, to an unlim-
ited depth and in respect of those mineral substances for which the exploitation 
permit is granted. The permit is valid for maximum 25 years and can be ex-
tended upon request by the permit holder for maximum periods of 15 years per 
extension. When the permit expires and no request for extension is filed, a mine 
closure plan must be submitted. In the event that the permit holder has not 
commenced the exploitation works within 12 months of the date at which the 
permit was granted, the council of ministers can decide to withdraw such permit. 

At each stage of the mining rights acquisition process several steps are required (e.g. applica-
tion, "publication", registration/filling payments, notarization, tax payments) before the applica-
tion is converted to an authorization or permit. A full description of the process is documented 
in the "Congo Code Minier" (2005) /1/. 

4 Accessibility, Climate, Local Resources, Infrastructure, 
and Physiography 

4.1 Accessibility 

Access to the Property is best from Pointe Noire, which is serviced by several direct interna-
tional flights from Paris and Johannesburg, or with local flights from the capital Brazzaville. 
From Pointe Noire the national road 1 (RN-1) goes directly in the Property. The road distance to 
the Holle mine on the eastern boundary of the property is in the range of 50km from the city 
centre of Pointe Noire. The single way railway line from Pointe Noire to Brazzaville passes cen-
tral from SW to NE through the Property. The fixed line telephone system in the RoC is rudi-
mentary only, but several operators offer mobile telephone services, which cover large areas of 
the Property  

4.2 Climate, Vegetation and Fauna 

The Property is located in the Congo coastal basin and has a tropical climate characterised by 
high humidity and heat. The average temperature is about 25°C and is up to 2°C higher at the 
end of the wet season and 2°C lower in the mid dry season. The dry season is from mid May 



 
 
 
 
Technical  Scoping  Study, Makola Exploration License, Kouilou Region, Republic of Congo 
EGB 10-012 
 
 

 
Page 21 of 97 

 

ERCOSPLAN Ingenieurgesellschaft Geotechnik und Bergbau mbH 
Arnstaedter Strasse 28, 99096 Erfurt, Germany 

 

Phone: + 49 361 3810 220  Fax: +49 361 3810 402 e-mail: mining@ercosplan.com web: http://www.ercosplan.com 
 

O:\@Projekte EGB aktuell\EGB_10-012_MagIndustries_Makola_DeskStudy\03proj_AUSLIEFERUNG\NI43-101\Final_Report\2010_08_MagIndustries_Makola-rev-01.docx 

until mid October with virtually no precipitation and the wet season is from mid October until 
mid May with an average over 150mm of precipitation per month3, usually as short but strong 
showers.   

The vegetation within the Property consists mainly of tall grasses and eucalyptus plantations 
on the higher plateaus and of tropical forest and agricultural plots in the valleys. The fauna 
consists of small mammals, reptiles and birds on the plateau areas, where in more remote 
parts monkeys and antelopes can exist, but were not observed during site visits. In the valleys, 
which are often swampy, small amphibians can also be found. 

4.3 Physiography 

The Property consist of a slowly rising undulating plateau rising from sea level to heights of 
200m in the northwest of the area. The plateau is intersected by river valleys and steep recent 
erosion gullies, which are 50 to 90m below the plateau. The SE eastern part of the property is 
dominated by the relatively flat and low lying Loeme river valley and contains the two large 
lakes of Loufoualéba and Bindi.  

4.4 Local Resources and Infrastructure 

The Property contains the RN-1 road, which recently has been reconstructed by Chinese con-
tractors and is in a reasonable good state. A large number of smaller unpaved tracks and 
roads are present, which offer good opportunities for transport he dry season using a 4x4 vehi-
cle, but are sometimes inaccessible during the wet season.  

The railway line from Pointe Noire to Brazzaville runs through the Property and is in this section 
of its length in a serviceable condition and can be used for transport of material. 

The on-shore and off-shore oil industry also produces associated natural gas. At the D'jeno oil 
terminal, located south west of the license area, gas is separated from oil and used to fire an 
on-site gas fired power plant. Pipelines were constructed recently from D'jeno to deliver treated 
natural gas to a further power plant near Pointe Noire and to the MAGMINERALS plant site near 
Mengo-  

MAGMINERALS maintains a permanent drilling camp near the village of Mengo with office and 
living facilities for about 120 people, some mechanical and electrical workshop facilities, inter-
net communication and in house generated electrical energy. Water is supplied by a well at the 
camp. Bottled water for human consumption is transported to the camp.  

The nearby city of Pointe Noire, which is the basis of the ROC off-shore and on-shore oil indus-
try, provides mechanical and technical services required for the exploration campaign and also 
for production. Its good harbour will facilitate easy transport of equipment during the construc-
tion phase and shipping of the finished potash product during the production phase.  

The ROC has a relatively high literacy compared to its neighbouring countries, but is lacking 
adequately educated technical specialists. The oil industry, which is the main industry in the

5 History 

This chapter first describes the history of potash exploration in the Kouilou region of ROC and 
then concentrates on the project history of MAGMINERALS potash operation.  

5.1 Potash Exploration in Congo 

Exploration for potash in the Kouilou region of the Congo basin started in the 1960's by the 
SYNDICAT DE RECHERCHES DE POTASSE AU CONGO with over 80 vertical wells over the 
Kouilou region as previous exploration for oil had shown the existence of potash salts. The goal 
of this exploration was not Carnallite potash ore, but Sylvite ore and based on this exploration 
the Holle Sylvite Mine was opened in 1969 (FEUGA ET AL., 2005 /12/).  

The data from this exploration campaign were investigated by reviewing the MDPA Holle mine 
archive originally retained in Wittelsbach (France) by MINES DE POTASSE D'ALSACE (MDPA) 
a daughter company of ENTREPRISE MINIERE ET CHIMIQUE (EMC). The data from this ar-
chive are the main part of the database available for the Makola Licence. Unfortunately, drill 
programs for oil exploration in the same area did not maintain details of the target salt se-
quence and provide only some rarely useable data. Seismic investigations were available but 
not useful due to being low resolution paper prints.  

After flooding of the Holle mine in 1977 (FEUGA ET AL., 2005  /12/), a further exploration cam-
paign for potash was started in the Kouilou region from the late 1980s to the early 1990s by 
MDPA (1989  /21/, 1992  /22/) for SOCIETE DES POTASSES DU CONGO (SPC). This campaign 
focussed on Sylvinite potash ore, but resources of Carnallite ore were also considered. This 
exploration campaign did not result in the planning or development of any mining projects. The 
data of production wells and the seismic investigations of this campaign were available for re-
view. 

In 1997 MAGNESIUM ALLOY CORPORATION (then known as CONGO MINERALS INC., which 
are former names of the MAGINDUSTRIES CORPORATION the parent company of MAGMIN-
ERALS) signed an exploration research agreement with the Congolese Ministry of Mines for an 
area which covered much of the Kouilou area including the area previously explored and de-
veloped for sylvitite and carnallitite. In 1998 MAGNESIUM ALLOY CORPORATION contracted 
SALZGITTER ANLAGENBAU AG to complete an independent technical and economic feasibil-
ity study for the operation of a magnesium smelter (SALZGITTER ANLAGENBAU, 1999  /19/). At 
this time the feasibility study did not consider the co-production of potash. During the feasibility 
study program slim hole Mengo 1 was drilled and studied in detail (MAGNESIUM ALLOY COR-
PORATION, 1998  /18/). 



 
 
 
 
Technical  Scoping  Study, Makola Exploration License, Kouilou Region, Republic of Congo 
EGB 10-012

Phone: + 49 361 3810 220  Fax: +49 361 3810 402 e-mail: mining@ercosplan.com web: http://www.ercosplan.com 
 

O:\@Projekte EGB aktuell\EGB_10-012_MagIndustries_Makola_DeskStudy\03proj_AUSLIEFERUNG\NI43-101\Final_Report\2010_08_MagIndustries_Makola-rev-01.docx 

The more recent exploration history of MAGMINERALS and its predecessors is described in 
section 5.2, and the data of these historical and recent drilling campaigns provide the basis for 
this resource estimate. 

Since 2009 a number of different companies announced plans for potash exploration in the 
Congo Coastal Basin (e.g. ELEMENTAL MINERALS, CONGO POTASH COMPANY, HOLLE-
POTASH), but up to present there are no indications that any exploration has been done by 
these companies and no new exploration results have been reported.  

5.2 Project History  

In 1999, a feasibility study for a 60,000tpa magnesium plant using brine obtained from solution 
mining of the Kouilou carnallite deposit was commissioned by MAGNESIUM ALLOY CORPO-
RATION a predecessor of the parent company of MAGMINERALS and completed by SALZGIT-
TER ANLAGENBAU (1999  /25/). As part of this study, a first exploration well was drilled, core 
samples were obtained and dissolution tests conducted.  

In mid 2004, a bankable feasibility study for a 69,000tpa magnesium plant was initiated. As part 
of this project, a contract was issued to GEA MESSO GMBH (MESSO) for the design of the 
Carnallite crystallization area, including the transformation of spent electrolyte into potash, 
considered as a by-product. Based on preliminary mass balance calculations, approximately 
120,000 tonnes per year of potash would be produced in this manner. A contract was also is-
sued to ERCOSPLAN for the design of the solution mining portion of the plant and for a Carnal-
lite reserve estimation using the available data and 5 new exploration wells. GENIVAR was pro-
vided with a mandate to overview the work of the contractors, in addition to providing the re-
quired engineering for remaining plant areas, which included infrastructure, gas handling and 
utility supply. 

During this period, through review of market information for both, magnesium metal and pot-
ash, and through discussions with potential product off-takers, it was concluded that the quan-
tities of potash that may be sold should be increased due to favourable future market growth 
and therefore product price. At the same time, the higher capital needed for the magnesium 
metal plant and the limited market growth for the metal, made the implementation of the metal 
plant more challenging. In early 2005, it was thus decided to change the project time line. Pot-
ash would now be produced as a first step in the longer term goal of producing magnesium 
metal and pot-ash. The initial tonnage for the potash plant was decided to be 400,000 tonnes 
with the MgCl2 effluent from the potash plant later being used for magnesium production. The 
MESSO and ERCOSPLAN mandates were modified accordingly. In parallel, Norbert Grüschow 
of NGCONSULTING, also conducted a scoping study for the potash plant to determine its 
technical viability  /23/. 

Based on the results of N. Grüschow's scoping study  /23/, in May 2005, MAGINDUSTRIES 
awarded GENIVAR a mandate to prepare a Phase 1 Bankable Feasibility Study (BFS) for the 
construction of a 400,000tpa potash plant in the vicinity of the planned magnesium plant in the 
Kouilou province, Republic of Congo. As for the magnesium plant, cold (ambient) solution min-
ing was to be used to produce the plant feed brine. In the course of the potash plant Phase 1 
BFS study, it was decided to conduct the study in two stages, whereas stage 1 would provide 
MAGINDUSTRIES with an interim report including preliminary capital, operating cost and cash-
flow analyses (GENIVAR, 2006,  /13/), and stage 2 would then take the study to financial close.

During the completion of stage 1, optimisation to reduce capital cost and increase revenues 
was initiated. Hot solution mining was identified as a process alternative that could generate 
significantly more product with only a small capital cost increase. The potential brine composi-
tion and temperature were estimated and confirmed by ERCOSPLAN, and an extension was 
awarded to the MESSO contract, in order to modify the plant design for this alternative. In par-
allel, ERCOSPLAN was requested to modify their design for the solution mining to accommo-
date the hot solution mining principle. The hot solution mining option increased the capacity to 
600,000tpa. Based on the studied available data ERCOSPLAN advised MAGINDUSTRIES that 
a 2D seismic survey as well as a total of at least 12 new exploration wells were required to be 
able to get proven reserves for a 20 years lifetime of the project in the Mengo area. The 2D 
seismic survey was undertaken by the DEUTSCHE MONTAN TECHNOLOGIE GMBH (DMT) in 
2006  /8/ and the drilling program was extended to 12 exploration wells. 

The original resource report by ERCOSPLAN (2007) /9/ for the Mengo area, was updated sev-
eral times ERCOSPLAN (2008) /10/, ERCOSPLAN (2009) /11/, because new rock mechanical 
studies became available and the outline of the brine field changed. Presently 25 of the re-
quired 50 exploitation drill holes for the brine field near Mengo have been completed.   

5.3 Historical Resource Estimates 

It should be noted that the resource estimates reported in this section of the Report do not al-
ways meet the criteria of NI 43-101 and are reported for historical purposes only.  

For the former Holle Mine, which is located within the Makola permit area, several historical 
resource estimates are available. The resource estimates are given for an area of approximately 
28 km², but for Sylvite only and do not include Carnallite resources.  

Resources of 22 million tonnes and 26 million tonnes of K2O have been reported for the 
layer 3 of cycle IX ,respectively by FEUGA ET AL. (2005)/12/ and DE RUITER (1979) /4/.  

DE RUITER (1979) /4/ reports additional 17 million tonnes of K2O resources in the layers 7 
and 8 of cycle IX. 

Based on the available data at the time these resource estimates are considered adequate, 
however, over the production time of the Holle mine from 1969 to 1977 with a total production 
of 3,184,603 tonnes of KCl (DE RUITER, 1979, /4/), it became clear that the structure of the de-
posit was more complex than originally thought. Therefore these resource estimates are 
probably too high.  

For the concession area of the Holle mine of 1300km² (boundaries unknown) FEUGA ET AL. 
(2005) /12/, report a total K2O resource (Carnallite and Sylvite) of 30 billion tonnes of K2O.  

Based on over 140 drill holes in the basin and on numerous seismic surveys as well as on in-
formation from the flooded Holle mine the Congolese government estimated Carnallite re-
sources of 800 billion tonnes4 for the complete Congo coastal basin. The Makola License area 
of 1,111km² encompasses 10 to 15 % of this basin.  

Compared to resources as defined by CIM (2005) /1/, the estimates for the Holle Sylvite mine 
can eventually be compared to indicated to measured resources, taking into account the num-

6 Geological Setting 

The geologic setting first describes the regional geology of the Congo coastal basin, before 
focussing on the structure and potential deposit horizons in the Makola property and discuss-
ing potential anomalies.  

6.1 Regional Geology 

Geologically, the deposit area is located in the Congo coastal basin of Aptian age (lower Cre-
taceous), which extends from Equatorial Guinea in the North, to Angola in the South (FEUGA 
ET AL., 2005  /12/). The basin represents the eastern flank of the pre-Atlantic continental rift ba-
sin which continues on the opposite coast of the South Atlantic Ocean in the Sergipe Basin, 
Brazil (e.g. DAVISON 2007  /3/), where large potash deposits are still under extraction and ex-
ploration. To the east this basin is bordered by Precambrian crystalline rocks, known as the 
Mayombe basement. 

6.1.1 Lithostratigraphy  

Because of intensive exploration for oil and potash, the stratigraphy of the basin is well known. 
In general, the thicknesses of the sedimentary strata changes from larger thicknesses along 
the coast to less thick strata towards east close to the Mayombe basement. From the bottom 
(older strata) to the top (younger strata), the following formations are differentiated ( Figure 2): 

On top of the crystalline basement a series of grey-green sandstones with interbedded pyrite 
bearing green claystones, known as Cocobeach Formation (Série du Cocobeach) occurs. The 
contact between the Cocobeach series and the overlying evaporites is an unconformity.  

The evaporite sequence contains three individual formations, 

• the Salt Formation at the bottom, 

• the Anhydrite Formation 

and  

• the Dolomitic Limestone Formation  

at the top of the evaporite sequence. 

The Salt Formation (Série Salifère) is divided into up to eleven large depositional cycles, num-
bered by roman numbers. Each of this cycle consists of several alternations of the sequence 
rock salt, carnallitite and sometimes with layers rich in Bischofite and/or Tachyhydrite . 

The Série Salifère is overlain by the Anhydrite Formation (Série Anhydritique), which consists of 
alternating anhydrite and marls of up to 5m thickness. The anhydrite is variable in appearance 
varying from nodular, brecciated, or laminated to massive. The marls are blue-grey to black 
and are locally very clay rich.

Figure 2  Stratigraphy of the Congo Coastal Basin, simplified after exploration well K3 
(SCHLUND & VANDENBROUCKE, 1960  /20/) 

The Anhydrite Formation is overlain by the Dolomitic Limestone Formation, which consists of 
several kinds of more or less dolomitisised limestones with some layers of marl and sand. The 
contact to the overlying, the Grey-Blue Claystones, is gradational. 

The Grey-Blue Claystone Formation (Série Argilo-Gréso-Dolomitique) consists of grey-blue to 
grey-green claystones to siltstones and fine grained sand-stones with a dolomitic cement and 
some layers/lenses of limestone. The contact to the overlying Iron Bearing Sandstones is an 
unconformity.

O:\@Projekte EGB aktuell\EGB_10-012_MagIndustries_Makola_DeskStudy\03proj_AUSLIEFERUNG\NI43-101\Final_Report\2010_08_MagIndustries_Makola-rev-01.docx 

The Iron Bearing Sandstone Formation (Sables Grossiers Ferrugineux) consists of layers of 
relatively coarse grained sandstones, locally with iron concretions and separated by layers of 
plastic red clay strata. Occasionally, thin discontinuous lenses of gravel are observed. In the 
top of this series, phosphate horizons can be present. The unconformable contact of this series 
to the overlying Coloured Clays and Sands suggests that the region has been tectonically ac-
tive and that locally the phosphate horizons have been removed through erosion. 

The Coloured Clays and Sands Formation (Série de Cirques) consists of alternating layers of 
sand and clay, which through lateritisation range in colour from yellow, over pink to dark red. If 
the phosphate horizons are missing, it is not always possible to distinguish between the Col-
oured Clay and Sands and Iron Bearing Sandstones and all are then referred to as Iron Bearing 
Sandstones. 

Traditionally the surface sands are distinguished as a separate unit, but since they are just 
washed out or weathered Coloured Clays and Sands, they are often not separately mentioned. 

It is not possible to precisely establish the boundaries for the 4 upper formations from drill hole 
cuttings only.  

6.1.2 Tectonics 

The evaporite rocks in the Congo basin are generally flat lying with a slight (< 5°) dip to the 
southwest. The basin is divided into large blocks by approximately NW-SE striking fault zones 
(MDPA, 1989  /15/). Locally, as for example at the former Holle mine uplifted blocks between 
relatively closely spaced fault zones occur (MDPA, 1987 /20/, MDPA 1989 /21/). Furthermore, 
subordinate NNE-SSW striking faults have been identified, which from the presently available 
data do not seem to have substantial vertical offset. Based on the 80 historic drill holes within 
and near the Makola License area, 12 new 8.5" drill holes drilled by MagMinerals for explora-
tion of the Mengo Explotation License area, 25 pre-production drill holes, recently completed 
for the brine field, an combined aerial photographic and satellite image lineation interpretation 
(MDPA,1989 /21/) following areas have been identified with potential fault systems.  

The first large scale NW-SE striking fault system is located in the southern part of the 
Makola License area close to drill hole LM1bis and separates the central part Makola li-
cense area with the Mengo area from the coastal area with Point Indienne and Pointe 
Noire. The fault is interpreted as a normal fault with a down throw of the south east 
block (see Figure 3 and Figure 4).  

The second large scale NW-SE striking fault system is located in the northeastern part 
of the Makola License area and separates the central part of the Makola License area 
with the Holle Mine from a lower lying block in the north east. The structure is inter-
preted as a steep reverse fault with the relative down throw of the south east block. A 
fault of this structure with up to 200m throw was drilled through in drill holes K28 (DE 
VAUCORBEIL H. & SCHLUND, J.M., 1982 /6/). This fault is not well defined in the northern 
corner of the license area (see Figure 3).  

Based on these large scale fault structures the Makola License area has been divided into 3 
zones, with zone 1 in the NE, zone 2 in the centre and zone 3 in the SW (0). 

The combination of the lineation interpretation with a detailed analysis of the thickness distribu-
tion of different potash horizons and the dip gradient of selected surfaces (e.g. top salt and top 



 
 
 
 
Technical  Scoping  Study, Makola Exploration License, Kouilou Region, Republic of Congo 
EGB 10-012 
 
 

 
Page 29 of 97

O:\@Projekte EGB aktuell\EGB_10-012_MagIndustries_Makola_DeskStudy\03proj_AUSLIEFERUNG\NI43-101\Final_Report\2010_08_MagIndustries_Makola-rev-01.docx 

of potash horizons) allowed to identify also three minor fault systems (Figure 3) with a throw in 
the range of a few 10m.  

The first smaller scale fault system is located in the northern part of the Makola License 
area south east of the NW-SE striking fault system and runs approximately NW-SE in 
the northern part and WNW-ESE in the southern part. This structure is interpreted as 
northward dipping reverse fault, which uplifts most of the Holle mine block relative to the 
central part of the Makola Licence area. The deep reaching dissolution structure that 
was cross cut during mining and resulted in flooding of the Holle mine could be located 
on this structure.   

The second smaller scale fault runs NE- SW and crosscuts the Mengo Exploitation li-
cence area, south of the intensively explored area. and can be followed to the first 
smaller scale fault system. The off-set is not very clear but is probably in the range of 
several 10m. 

The third smaller scale fault runs NW-SE just North of the Mengo Exploitation area and 
can be followed to the NW over the boundary of the Makola Permit and to the SE about 
8 km beyond the Mengo Exploitation area, further to the SE its location is not well de-
fined. The off-set is not very clear but is probably in the range of several 10m. 

For the last two smaller scale faults systems, the seismic studies of DMT (2007, /8/) have 
shown the existence of small scale fault systems with comparable orientations in the well ex-
plored part of this area. 

6.1.3 Hydrogeology  

During the exploration in the 1960's culminating in opening of the Holle mine, a number of hy-
drogeologic studies has been conducted (e.g. VAUCORBEIL ET AL., 1963 /7/, LEMAIRE, 1965 /16/, 
etc.) to identify a potential water source for the plant operation. Also for the MagMinerals explo-
ration project near Mengo some hydrogeological studies were implemented.    

All studies come to the same conclusions that a first shallow aquifer is present at a shallow 
depth in coarse to middle grained sand. Further down, groundwater is pressurized and can be 
encountered in coarse sand lenses in this formation, especially towards the Dolomitic Lime-
stone Formation. The base of the Grey Blue Clay Formation, rich in dolomite sand has been 
encountered as a zone of mud losses. The strongly jointed Dolomitic Limestone Formation 
should also be considered as a potential aquifer in the succession (MDPA, 1987  /20/). In con-
trast to experience gained from other evaporite basins, the contact of the Salt Formation to the 
Anhydrite Formation is generally dry here.  

Test work on some wells in the 1960's and 2007, indicated that the productivity of these aqui-
fers, was not adequate to ensure at all times adequate process water to the plant and mining 
operation.

6.1.4 Local Geology of Potash Bearing Horizons 

In the Makola permit area the geology is comparable to the regional geology. Within most drill 
holes of the permit at least nine of the eleven (11) cycles of the salt formation occur. In general 
the marl rich rock salt that separates the cycles can be correlated between the drill holes and 
allows a coarse division of the salt section. Furthermore the cycle X contains in its lower part a 
thick (~60m) horizon of rock salt with occasional anhydrite/clay marker beds, which can be 
very well correlated between the drill holes. The unequivocal assignment of a carnallitite layer 
to a certain Carnallite "couche" as is done in the MDPA system, is not always possible as some 
cycles are not complete or not enough Carnallite layers are present. For this reason carnallitite 
mineralisation potential suitable for mining (horizons) have been defined that  

1. can be correlated between several drill holes and can be used to interpolate the lateral 
extent of such Horizon, 

2. the thickness of the carnallitite horizon shoudl be in excess of 4m as thinner layers can-
not be efficiently mined, locally the combined thickness of two or more carnallitite layers 
("couches") separated only by relatively thin rock salt layers have been combined in 
one horizon.  

.A comparison of the MDPA nomenclature (1989,  /21/), (1992, /22/) and the nomenclature (for 
mineable carnallitite horizons) used in this report is given in  Table 4.

6.2 Geological Anomalies 

Every process affecting the normal character of the carnallitite horizons, results in a geological 
anomaly. Each geological anomaly of a carnallitite horizon can affect its suitability for mining. In 
general three types of geological anomalies can be distinguished for potash deposits: 

1. non-deposition of carnallitite 

2. displacement of the carnallitite layer along a fault zone 

3. dissolution or displacement of the carnallitite after deposition 

 

(1) The non-deposition of a carnallitite layer can often be identified by comparing the lithologi-
cal logs of several drill holes. The non-deposition is identified, when in an overall normal profile 
a carnallitite layer is missing, without visible disturbance of underlying or overlying layers. The 
interpretation is supported, when the logs of several drill holes show a gradual thinning or 



 
 
 
 
Technical  Scoping  Study, Makola Exploration License, Kouilou Region, Republic of Congo 
EGB 10-012 
 
 

 
Page 35 of 97 

 

ERCOSPLAN Ingenieurgesellschaft Geotechnik und Bergbau mbH 
Arnstaedter Strasse 28, 99096 Erfurt, Germany 

 

Phone: + 49 361 3810 220  Fax: +49 361 3810 402 e-mail: mining@ercosplan.com web: http://www.ercosplan.com 
 

O:\@Projekte EGB aktuell\EGB_10-012_MagIndustries_Makola_DeskStudy\03proj_AUSLIEFERUNG\NI43-101\Final_Report\2010_08_MagIndustries_Makola-rev-01.docx 

thickening between the logs. The present database for the Makola Licence area, allows  to 
identify this kind of anomalies for example for cycle VIII, couche 4, which increases in thickness 
from <1m in K25 (DE VAUCORBEIL & SCHLUND, 1962 /5/) in the east and increases in thickness to 
10m westward in Mengo 13 (ERCOSPLAN, 2009 /11/) or K17 (SCHLUND & DE VAUCORBEIL, 1961 
/27/). 

(2) Within the Makola permit a number of significant fault zones have been identified (section 
6.1.2). The displacement of a carnallitite layer along a fault has the result that if the fault was not 
a pathway for fluid flow, the carnallitite layer is still there, but at another level as expected. This 
might result in large mining losses. However, because faults are often pathways for fluid trans-
port geological anomalies of type 2 and 3 often occur together. 

(3) For the dissolution of the carnallitite after deposition two varieties must be identified: 

(i) If the amount of water involved in the dissolution or displacement is relatively low, the 
carnallitite is displaced by either sylvinite or coarse crystalline rock salt, which can often 
be identified in profile, but should not be misinterpreted as non-deposition. The Sylvite 
deposit the Holle mine mined is interpreted to be such anomaly.   

(ii) If the amount of water involved is large, subrosion takes place. Subrosion is the 
process by which soluble rocks in the underground are dissolved by groundwater or 
water penetrating from the surface. Subrosion can take place where soluble rocks are 
not protected by a layer of impermeable rocks. This can either be at the border of a ba-
sin, where the local dip of the layers allows water access to the soluble rocks or along 
tectonic structures (e.g. faults), where water is brought into contact with soluble rocks 
below the impermeable layer. The consequence of the subrosion process is the devel-
opment of a fluid filled cavity in the underground. With ongoing subrosion the size of the 
cavity will increase. Depending on the dimension of the cavity, on the depth at which it 
develops as well as on the properties of the overlying rocks several structures can de-
velop ranging from large shallow depressions to deep steep walled pits if the overlying 
rocks collapse into the cavity. This kind of structure has been identified near the former 
Holle mine as source of the water inflow that resulted in loss of the mine (e.g. FEUGA ET 
AL., 2005 /12/). No other such structures have been unequivocally identified, but it 
should be assumed that more of such structures are present. 

These kinds of geological anomalies are commonly encountered in potash mining operations 
and might have different consequences depending on the specific deposit and the character of 
the mining operation. When type 2 or 3 anomalies are identified a safety pillar is left between 
the planned mine workings/caverns and the anomaly structure to prevent negative influences 
on the operation. This reduces the mineable resources.

7 Deposit Type 

The term potash is usually used to describe "muriate of potash" (MOP) with the chemical for-
mula (KCl). Another potassium fertilizer is "sulfate of potash" (SOP) with the chemical formula 
K2SO4. To be able to compare these products the grade of potassium products is usually ex-
pressed in K2O (potassium oxide). The Kouilou potash project of MagMinerals proposes to 
produce KCl. A tonne of KCl contains an equivalent of 0.632 tonnes of K2O. 

The Congo coastal Basin contains 2 potash bearing minerals;  

• Sylvite, chemical formula KCl  

• Carnallite, chemical formula KMgCl3 x 6 H2O 

A rock consisting mainly of Sylvite together with Halite is called sylvinite. Subordinately anhy-
drite, clay and or dolomite can occur. The latter two are taken together as insoluble material in 
the assays.  

A rock consisting mainly of Carnallite, together with Halite is called carnallitite. Subordinately 
the highly soluble salt minerals Tachyhydrite and/or Bischofite as well as anhydrite, clay and or 
dolomite can occur.  

In general sylvinite deposits consist of relatively thin beds, with KCl content between 20 and 
35%, whereas carnallitite deposits usually consist of relatively thick beds with KCl content be-
tween 8 and 24%.  

The sylvinite in the Congo coastal basin is usually secondary (MDPA, 1989 /21/) and requires 
that the original carnallitite deposit type has reacted with water according to the reaction:  

Carnallite + Water = Sylvite + MgCl2 rich brine. 

The presence of water in the carnallitite salt rocks is a consequence of fault zones or other dis-
turbances present. The sylvinite deposits in the Congo coastal basin, therefore, occur only lo-
cally and are mostly structurally controlled (MDPA, 1989 /21/). In the Makola Exploration Li-
cense sylvinite occurs along the Holle mine structure, where it was conventionally mined, till 
the mine flooded in 1977 (FEUGA ET AL., 2005 /12/). 

The carnallitite in the Congo Coastal Basin, which is the dominant deposit type, is considered 
primary. Single carnallitite horizons can be traced over up to 30km (MDPA, 1992 /22/). There-
fore the geological model for the deposit considers that the carnallitite occurs in subhorizontal 
layers, extending over large areas, which are only locally disturbed.

8 Mineralization 

Within the Makola License, potash mineralisation occurs either as sylvinite or as carnallitite. A 
survey of the drill hole descriptions showed that within the available drill holes a sylvinite min-
eralisation that can be correlated between several drill holes only occurs within (i) in the Holle 
mine area in zone 2 and (ii) in zone 3 between the drill holes K 04, K22 and Kou 11, outside the 
license area. Because the sylvinite mineralisation in the Holle mine area is secondary as result 
of a reaction with groundwater with the carnallitite, resulting in a decrease in thickness, the 
definition of potential mining horizons will be based on the carnallitite horizons only.  

A carnallitite mineralisation suitable for mining should meet the following criteria: 

(1) The carnallitite horizons should be present in several bore holes to be able to in-
terpolate the lateral extent of the carnallitite layer, 

(2) The thickness of the carnallitite horizon should be in excess of 4m as thinner 
carnallitite layers cannot be efficiently mined, 

(3) The average Carnallite content of the material should be > 40%,  

Based on the first two criteria, seven carnallitite horizons potential suitable for mining are identi-
fied in the Makola License area: 

- Horizon 1: Carnallite level 7 of the cycle X with a thickness range from less than 1m 
in the Holle mine area, to about 20m in the Mengo sector (Figure 5). 

- Horizon 2: Carnallite level 3 of the cycle X with a thickness range from less than 1m 
in the Holle mine area, to about 14m in the Mengo sector (Figure 6). 

- Horizon 3: Carnallite level 3 of the cycle IX with a regular thickness from near 5m in 
average in the Holle mine area (where this was the main mined sylvinite Horizon) to 
more than 10m in the Mengo sector and near Pointe Noire area (Figure 7). 

- Horizon 4: Carnallite levels 3 and 2 of the cycle VIII with a regular thickness from 
about 6m in the Holle mine area to 15m in the Mengo sector and in the Pointe Noire 
area (Figure 8). 

These first 4 horizons were the goal of MagIndustries exploration program near the village of 
Mengo and the definition is the same as in the resource and reserve reports of ERCOSPLAN 
(e.g. ERCOSPLAN, 2009, /11/).  

- Horizon 5: Carnallite level 7 of the cycle VII with a thickness range from 4 m in the 
Holle Mine and Mengo area, but upto 8 m in Pointe Noire (Figure 9) 

- Horizon 6: Carnallite level 1 of the cycle IV with a thickness in the range of 10m over 
most of the permit area (Figure 10). 

- Horizon 7: Carnallite level 1 of the cycle II with a thickness in the range of 8 to 10m in 
the SW part of the Permit and a thickness of 10 to 12m in the NE part of the permit area 
(Figure 11). 

The geologic model shows that the potential mineable potash horizons identified are continu-
ous over most of the permit area and that resources can be estimated from them using a rela-
tively large area of confidence.

9 Exploration 

The potash exploration and mining history of the Makola License area has been described in 
chapter 5. A list with potash exploration and mining activities in the Makola License area is 
given in Table 5.  

No further potash exploration activities are known to have occurred in the license area. The 
historical exploration drilling with data available about potash mineralisation will be summa-
rized in chapter 10.  

For this study no additional exploration activities have been conducted.

10 Drilling 

No new drilling for potash exploration has been done in the Makola exploration license area 
since the 1990's. Between 2005 and 2008 MAGINDUSTRIES concluded an exploration cam-
paign near the village of Mengo, which is surrounded by the Makola license area and provides 
important constraints on the geological model for the Makola License area. All exploration drill 
holes important for a geological model of the Makola License area are listed in Table 6.  

MAGINDUSTIES, furthermore, has brought down 25 exploitation drill holes in their Mengo ex-
plotation licence area. These drill holes in the vicinity of MENGO 10 confirm the geological 
model, but the polygons constructed around these drill holes do not extend to the Makola Li-
cense area to provide resources and, therefore have not been listed separately in Table 6.

For the K and Kou series holes the salt series was continuously cored and the interpreted re-
sults are shown in the Appendix B. These holes were drilled using a magnesiumchloride brine 
as drilling fluid, which is adequate to obtain reliable carnallitite cores, if no Tachyhydrite or 
Bischofite is present in the deposit.  

The Mengo series holes were spot cored only and the Carnallite content was estimated from 
geophysical logging results. The results are shown in the Appendix B. Most holes were drilled 
using a magnesiumchloride brine as drilling fluid, but Mengo 5B, Mengo 7 and Mengo 11 were 
drilled using Oil Based Mud, which has the advantage it does also not react with Tachyhydrite 
and Bischofite.  

For the other drill holes the drilling procedures were not reported/available, but the interpreted 
results are shown in Appendix B. 

The deposit being more or less subhorizontal, the sample length is in general the true thick-
ness of the mineralisation.  

11 Sampling Method and Approach 

The data used in this study with exception of the data from the Mengo drill holes (procedure 
described in ERCOSPLAN 2009, /11/) are historical data and no protocol about the sampling 
method and approach have been reported.  

12 Sample Preparation, Analysis and Security 

For the K series and KOU series samples for assaying were taken from the core, but no de-
scription of the procedure has been found. The samples were transported to France for analy-
sis in the laboratory of MDPA in Wittelsheim (France). Sample preparation is not described but 
it can be inferred from reported results that a weighted amount of sample was dissolved in a 
defined volume of water. The insoluble content was determined by weighing the amount of 
material that was not dissolved after a certain time.  

There is no detail given about the analytical procedure, but the reported results suggest that a 
wet chemical procedure with independent determination of the single chemical components 
was used. All samples were at least analysed for K, Mg, and Cl to obtain the K2O content and 
an indication whether the potash mineral present is either Carnallite or Sylvite. Some samples

were further analysed for SO4, Br and Ca. Sodium content was estimated over mass or ionic 
balance.  

There is therefore no information about the measures taken to ensure the validity and integrity 
of sampling and assaying results including: 

(a) if any aspect of the sample preparation was conducted by an employee, officer, di-
rector, or associate of the issuer; 

(b) Details regarding sample preparation, assaying  and analytical procedures used in-
cluding sub-sample size and whether the analytical or testing laboratories are certi-
fied by any standard association and the particulars of certification (the MDPA labo-
ratory in Wittelsheim, at that time, was associated with a producing mine, and there-
fore highly experienced in analysis of potash salts);  

or  

(c)  A summary of the nature and extent of quality control measures employed and 
check assay or other check analytical and testing procedures utilized, including the 
results and corrective actions taken.  

Because of lack of information concerning adequacy of sampling, sample preparation, security 
and analytical procedures, it is not possible for the authors to offer an opinion as to these as-
pects other than to state that the examination of the analytical results suggests that the proce-
dures were consistent, in the authors experiences, with the procedures used in other projects 
at that time.   

13 Data Verification 

National Instrument 43-101 and Exploration Best Practices Guidelines state that a program of 
data verification should accompany an exploration program to confirm validity of exploration 
data (CIM, 2005, /1/). Furthermore, the guidelines require a quality assurance quality control 
(QA/QC) program to be in place. 

As the study uses only historical data and no stored core material or other deposit material was 
available, independent verification of the reported results and QA/QC was not possible. 

Because of the incomplete nature of the reported analysis with sodium determined from the 
ionic balance, this methodology could not be used as internal check on quality of the analysis.

14 Adjacent Properties 

The Makola Exploration License area surrounds the Mengo Exploitation License held by MAG-
MINERALS and borders on the  

Loango Exploration License held by MAGMINERALS to the W, 

Manenga Exploration License held AFRIMINES (Holle Potash Company) to the SE, 

Tchitondi Exploration License held by AFRIMINES to the NE, 

and 

Mboukoumassi Exploration License Area held by ZHENGWEI TECHNIQUE CONGO to 
the N. 

The Mengo Exploitation License area has been well investigated (ERCOSPLAN, 2009 /11/) and 
MAGMINERALS is developing a carnallitite solution mining and brine processing operation 
there.   

For the Loango Exploration License to the East, MAGMINERALS is developing an exploration 
concept, based on the evaluation of historical exploration data in that area. There is no informa-
tion available about active exploration activities of other companies in the license area sur-
rounding the Makola License area.  

15 Mineral Processing and Metallurgical Testing 

Although a mining method has not yet been defined for the deposit rocks information about 
test work on some typical mined ores or brines is available from historical and recent sources.  

The conventional Holle mine used a flotation process to separate the Sylvite from the Halite 
and insoluble material in the ore. Flotation tests were run in the 1960's to optimise the process 
(e.g. MDPA, 1961 /19/, WEINIG, 1965 /28/). WEINIG (1965) /28/, also describes also test work 
using heavy liquid separation, but to reach acceptable recoveries with this method the material 
has to be ground very fine and this method was not further investigated. Based on the process-
ing test work by WEINIG (1965) the flotation circuit of the plant was designed and seemed to be 
adequate as there is no mention of problems with the flotation procedure of the ore in the 
available company intern documents from the Holle mine archive.   

With the beginning of the 1970's MDPA as the parent company of CPC operating the Holle 
mine investigated the use of conventional mined Carnallite for potash production (e.g. LAG-
NEAU, 1972 /17/). In these studies several feasible processing routes were proposed with the 
recommendation for further studies, but no further information about processing tests on con-
ventional mined carnallitite from the Holle mine have been found. The processing of conven-
tional mined carnallitite ore is proven technology and well understood (e.g. SCHULZ ET AL., 2006 
/28/).  

There are two principle ways to produce KCl from a carnallitite ore: 

1. Cold Decomposition of the ore, which reacts the (pretreated) ore with a limited amount 
of water at ambient temperature to produces an MgCl2 rich brine and fine grained de-



 
 
 
 
Technical  Scoping  Study, Makola Exploration License, Kouilou Region, Republic of Congo 
EGB 10-012 
 
 

 
Page 52 of 97 

 

ERCOSPLAN Ingenieurgesellschaft Geotechnik und Bergbau mbH 
Arnstaedter Strasse 28, 99096 Erfurt, Germany 

 

Phone: + 49 361 3810 220  Fax: +49 361 3810 402 e-mail: mining@ercosplan.com web: http://www.ercosplan.com 
 

O:\@Projekte EGB aktuell\EGB_10-012_MagIndustries_Makola_DeskStudy\03proj_AUSLIEFERUNG\NI43-101\Final_Report\2010_08_MagIndustries_Makola-rev-01.docx 

composition Sylvite mixed with the non Carnallite part of the ore. To obtain a product of 
a saleable quality requires (i) pre-treatment of the ore to minimise the amount of in-
soluble material and Halite that will be mixed with the decomposition Sylvite or (ii) post 
treatment of the low grade decomposition Sylvite to produce a high quality product. 
The pre-treatment consisting either of reverse NaCl flotation or heavy media separation 
is preferred as the only feasible post treatment option would be hot leaching, which 
would introduce an energy intensive processing step. The overall efficiency of a cold 
decomposition process with pre-treatment of the ore, without additional evaporation 
steps would be in the range of 75% recovery of KCl from the ore. 

2. "Hot" Leaching of the ore, which dissolves Carnallite and some Halite. The resulting 
brine brine is heated and concentrates through evaporation with crystallisation of some 
Halite. KCl is crystallised from the concentrated brine through cooling. The cooled 
brine is partly recycled to dissolve further ore and the cycle continues. The crystallised 
KCl can be beneficiated to a saleable K60 product by washing. This process can have 
an overall efficiency of in the order of 90% recovery of KCl from the ore, depending on 
the exact layout of the process and the availability of energy for evaporation. 

Both processes produce relative large volumes of MgCl2 brine that have to be disposed of. 
Test work on carnallitite from the deposit is required for validation and optimisation of both 
processing options.  

For the planned potash operation based on solution mining of carnallitite ore near Mengo from 
MagMinerals Potash Inc., mineral processing and mettalurgical test work has been done, con-
sisting of dissolution tests on carnallitite cores by NGCONSULTING (2007) /24/ and KCl crystalli-
sation test on brine from the dissolution testwork by HPD (2007) /14/. This test work on material 
from the deposit near Mengo has shown that the processing of brine from solution mining of 
carnallitite from the Congo coastal basin is feasible and indicates that also the carnallitite from 
other areas of the deposit can be solution mined and the resulting brine processed. The proc-
ess is described in ERCOSPLAN 2009 /11/. 

16 Mineral Resource and Mineral Reserve Estimates 

Mineral Resource" as defined by National Instrument 43-101 and CIM (2005) /1/ : 

A Mineral Resource is a concentration or occurrence of diamonds, natural solid inorgan-
ic material, or natural solid fossilized organic material including base and precious met-
als, coal, and industrial minerals in or on the Earth's crust in such form and quantity and 
of such a grade or quality that it has reasonable prospects for economic extraction. The 
location, quantity, grade, geological characteristics and continuity of a Mineral Resource 
are known, estimated or interpreted from specific geological evidence and knowledge.  

According to the definition of "Mineral Reserve" by the National Instrument 43-101 and CIM 
(2005) /1/: 

A Mineral Reserve is the economically mineable part of a Measured or Indicated Mineral 
Resource demonstrated by at least a Preliminary Feasibility Study. This Study must in-
clude adequate information on mining, processing, metallurgical, economic and other 
relevant factors that demonstrate, at the time of reporting, that economic extraction can
