be justified. A Mineral Reserve includes diluting materials and allowances for losses that 
may occur when the material is mined. 

This chapter starts with general assumptions and methodology of the resource and reserve 
estimate, followed by a description of the geological model, the estimation method, a discus-
sion of potential mining methods and the resource and reserve estimate. The metallurgical and 
processing aspects for different mining routes have been discussed in chapter 15. 

16.1 Assumptions and Methodology 

In determining the potential extent, quality, and volume of the mineral resource in the Makola 
Exploration Licence Area the authors were guided by principles for exploration and sampling 
techniques commonly used in the world-wide potash industry for Carnallite exploration as well 
as by the guidelines established by CIM in the NI 43-101 for reporting reserves and resources.  

o The primary tool employed to determine thickness and concentration of the Carnal-
lite mineralisation is drilling, with coring of potash bearing parts and wire line log-
ging of the whole salt sequence.  

o The potash concentration along the length of the mineralised Horizons is either de-
termined from assaying of samples or estimated from the geophysical wire line logs, 
which have been calibrated for the main potash bearing Horizons against the assay 
data. 

o The extent of the potash mineralisation and the continuity between drill holes can be 
determined by subsurface mapping, from 2D seismic lines or from correlation be-
tween drill holes.  

o For the estimation of mineral resources, the areal extent around the drill holes for 
which it is reasonable to infer the geological conditions depends on the continuity of 
the deposit and the radius for the confidence interval may vary from 10 meters to up 
to 2 miles.  

For the Makola License Area a geological model of the salt sequence was developed and used 
as basis for the resource estimate 

16.2 Geological Model 

Based on the 80 historic drill holes within and near the Makola License area, 12 new 8.5" drill 
holes drilled by MAGMINERALS for exploration of the Mengo Explotation License area, 25 pre-
production drill holes, recently completed for the brine field, an combined aerial photographic 
and satellite image lineation interpretation (MDPA;1989 /21/) as well as the authors' experience 
with Carnallite deposits, a geological model of the 1,111 km² license area and surrounding 
areas was developed. Overall the underground mapping based on the drill hole data indicates 
a layering of all horizons (including multiple carnallitite Horizons) in the salt which dip slightly to 
the NW.  

This slightly dipping layering is disturbed in a number of areas, where modeling of the salt hori-
zons and top of the salt have identified steep gradients in dip, which have been interpreted as 



 
 
 
 
Technical  Scoping  Study, Makola Exploration License, Kouilou Region, Republic of Congo 
EGB 10-012 
 
 

 
Page 54 of 97 

 

ERCOSPLAN Ingenieurgesellschaft Geotechnik und Bergbau mbH 
Arnstaedter Strasse 28, 99096 Erfurt, Germany 

 

Phone: + 49 361 3810 220  Fax: +49 361 3810 402 e-mail: mining@ercosplan.com web: http://www.ercosplan.com 
 

O:\@Projekte EGB aktuell\EGB_10-012_MagIndustries_Makola_DeskStudy\03proj_AUSLIEFERUNG\NI43-101\Final_Report\2010_08_MagIndustries_Makola-rev-01.docx 

potential fault zones. The combination of the lineation interpretation and these areas with steep 
gradients has been used to define 2 major NW-SE striking fault systems with locally a vertical 
throw of up to 100m, that divides the Makola License area in 3 zones.  

The first large scale NW-SE striking fault system is located in the southern part of the 
Makola Licence area close to drill hole LM1bis and separates the central part Makola li-
cence area with the Mengo area from the coastal area with Point Indienne and Pointe 
Noire. The fault is interpreted as a normal fault with a downthrow of the south east block 
(see Figure 3).  

The second large scale NW-SE striking fault system is located in the norteastern part of 
the Makola Licence area and seperates the central part of the Makola Licence area with 
the Holle Mine from a lower lying block in the north east. The structure is interpreted as 
a steep reverse fault with the relative downthrow of the south east block. This fault is not 
well defined in the northern corner of the license area (see Figure 3).  

The combination of the lineation interpretation with a detailed analysis of the thickness distribu-
tion of different potash horizons and the dip gradient of selected surfaces (e.g. top salt and top 
of potash horizons) allowed to identify a three minor fault systems with a throw in the range of a 
few 10m.  

The first smaller scale fault system is located in the northern part of the Makola License 
area south east of the NW-SE striking fault system and runs approximately NW-SE in 
the northern part and WNW-ESE in the southern part. This structure is interpreted as 
northward dipping reverse fault, which uplifts most of the Holle mine block relative to the 
central part of the Makola Licence area. The deep reaching dissolution structure that 
was cross cut during mining and resulted in flooding of the Holle mine could be located 
on this structure.   

The second smaller scale fault runs NE- SW and crosscuts the Mengo Exploitation li-
cence area, south of the intensively explored area. and can be followed to the first 
smaller scale fault system. The off-set is not very clear but is probably in the range of 
several 10m. 

The third smaller scale fault runs NW-SE just North of the Mengo Exploitation area and 
can be followed to the NW over the boundary of the Makola Permit and to the SE about 
8 km beyond the Mengo Exploitation area, further to the SE its location is not well de-
fined. The off-set is not very clear but is probably in the range of several 10m. 

For the last two smaller scale faults systems, the seismic studies of DMT (2007) /8/ have shown 
the existence of small scale fault systems with comparable orientations in the well explored part 
of this area. 

Within the blocks bordered by these interpreted fault zones, correlation over large distances 
between drill holes is possible, indicating undisturbed layering of the salt and, consequently of 
the deposit rocks. 

16.3 Estimation Method 

Taking into account the discussion in the previous chapters about the mineable carnallitite ho-
rizons 1 to 7 that can be inferred to be present over most of the licence area are used for the 



 
 
 
 
Technical  Scoping  Study, Makola Exploration License, Kouilou Region, Republic of Congo 
EGB 10-012 
 
 

 
Page 55 of 97 

 

ERCOSPLAN Ingenieurgesellschaft Geotechnik und Bergbau mbH 
Arnstaedter Strasse 28, 99096 Erfurt, Germany 

 

Phone: + 49 361 3810 220  Fax: +49 361 3810 402 e-mail: mining@ercosplan.com web: http://www.ercosplan.com 
 

O:\@Projekte EGB aktuell\EGB_10-012_MagIndustries_Makola_DeskStudy\03proj_AUSLIEFERUNG\NI43-101\Final_Report\2010_08_MagIndustries_Makola-rev-01.docx 

estimation of inferred resources, since there is a reasonable prospect for economical mining of 
these Horizons.  

Furthermore, some potentially mineable potash layers occur in single drill holes, but their exis-
tence cannot be inferred over larger parts of the licence area. These potash layers have also 
been used to estimate inferred resources, since at least locally within the Makola License area, 
there is a reasonable prospect for economical mining of these layers. 

In calculating the mineral resource tonnages, the following procedures were completed (re-
sources are given as mineralisation in place): 

(1) Around the inferred fault localities, exclusion zones of 100m width for the large 
scale faults and 50m width for the small scale faults were defined. 

(2) The area of the flooded Holle mine was defined as exclusion zone (the area was 
taken from a mine plan of 1977, the year the mine flooded and a 500m safety pil-
lar area was kept to the mine workings. 

(3) Around each drill hole with assay data for a Horizon or potash layer, an area of 
influence was defined, which could not extend beyond a fault zone.  

(4) For each such defined areal the remaining net area was determined. From these 
defined areas 35% was taken out for assumed non mineable areas in compari-
son with the technical and geological exclusion zone factor for inferred re-
sources as defined in ERCOSPLAN 2009 /11/. Based on the mine plans of the 
Holle mine, that showed substantial barren areas in the sylvinite ore zones, the 
factor is increased to 50% for the sylvinite ore.  

(5) The volume for each identified potash layer was calculated by multiplying the net 
remaining area with the thickness (defined by availability of assaying data) of the 
potential mining Horizon. 

(6) The volume calculated for each Horizon was multiplied with a carnallitite or syl-
vinite tonnage factor (density), which was determined individually for each area 
and for each layer from the K2O content of the layer assuming that all K is pre-
sent as Carnallite for carnallitite ore. This underestimates the ore mass in case of 
some K being present as Sylvite. In case of sylvinite ore it was assumed that all 
K is present as Sylvite. 

(7) The tonnage of ore resources was recalculated to KCl reserves by multiplication 
with the K2O content of the ore.  

For the resource and reserve estimation the drill holes as listed in Table 6 for which at least of 
some potash layers assaying data with K2O content are available have been used.  

The Report classifies the Carnallite mineralisation in terms of inferred resources only as defined 
by NI 43-101 in CIM (2005) /1/. This reflects the level of confidence in the extent and grade of 
the identified potash ore body. Only inferred resources are defined, because no core material 
was preserved from the historic drill holes and an independent QA/QC of the assaying was not 
possible. 

In the opinion of the authors, in the Kouilou Carnallite deposit in the Makola Licence area in-
ferred resources occur in a radius of 3,000m of a drill hole for a carnallitite ore layer that can be 
correlated with other drill holes over the license area and in a radius of 1500m of a drill holes 
for a potash layer that cannot be correlated over the license area or where the ore is sylvinite.   



 
 
 
 
Technical  Scoping  Study, Makola Exploration License, Kouilou Region, Republic of Congo 
EGB 10-012 
 
 

 
Page 56 of 97 

 

ERCOSPLAN Ingenieurgesellschaft Geotechnik und Bergbau mbH 
Arnstaedter Strasse 28, 99096 Erfurt, Germany 

 

Phone: + 49 361 3810 220  Fax: +49 361 3810 402 e-mail: mining@ercosplan.com web: http://www.ercosplan.com 
 

O:\@Projekte EGB aktuell\EGB_10-012_MagIndustries_Makola_DeskStudy\03proj_AUSLIEFERUNG\NI43-101\Final_Report\2010_08_MagIndustries_Makola-rev-01.docx 

The rational for these radii is that within the evaporites of the Congo coastal basin correlation of 
carnallitite Horizons and marker beds is straightforward, but local disruptions occur, as shown 
by the geological model. Within the fault defined blocks it can be assumed that variations are 
minor and gradual. For the Makola License area the results of the historical drilling campaign, 
show a near perfect correlation of the carnallitite Horizons and marker beds between the wells. 
The inferred resources for sylvinite have a smaller radius of confidence, as the reaction trans-
forming carnallitite to sylvinite is localized and that extrapolation away from a known locality 
becomes uncertain.  

A comparison with similar operations shows that this compares well to the method applied by 
MOSAIC for the Belle Plain solution mining facility in Saskatchewan, Cana-da and the Hershey 
solution mining facility in Michigan, USA, which state in their Form 10 K submission to the Unit-
ed States Securities and Exchange Commission (US SEC): "Proven reserves are estimated by 
identifying material in place that is delineated on at least two sides and material in-place within 
a half mile (0.804 km) radius or distance from an existing sampled mine entry or exploration 
core hole. Probable reserves are estimated by identifying material in place within a one mile 
radius or distance from an existing sampled mine entry or exploration core hole". The metho-
dology applied here for the calculation of "Proven" or Probable Reserves" are similar to the 
methods used to estimate "Measured" and "Indicated" resources with the nomenclature of NI 
43-101. Since the Mosaic 10 K submission to the US SEC uses the definition of a radius from a 
cored drill hole to establish reserves, the authors suggest that this precedent can be used to 
establish the distance from a drill hole. 

16.4 Mining Concepts 

In principle three different mining concepts can be considered: 

• Open pit or strip mining, 

• Conventional Underground Mining,  

• Underground Solution Mining. 

Each of these concepts will be shortly discussed below taking into account the circumstances 
of Kouilou potash deposit in the Makola License area.  

16.4.1 Open Pit or Strip Mining 

Open pit or strip mining are surface mining techniques, which require that the over-burden ma-
terial is removed from the ore and the ore is than excavated at the surface. Open pit mining is 
usually used for disseminated ores, whereas strip mining is used for layered ores. For the 
Kouilou potash deposit strip mining would be necessary.  

For strip mining the economic viability is determined by the strip ratio, commonly defined as 
cubic meters of waste (overburden) to tonnes of ore that can be mined. With the first potential 
mineable carnallitite horizon at minimum depths of 250m and the last mineable carnallitite hori-
zon at depths of 1000m, strip mining is not an economical feasible option.  



 
 
 
 
Technical  Scoping  Study, Makola Exploration License, Kouilou Region, Republic of Congo 
EGB 10-012 
 
 

 
Page 57 of 97 

 

ERCOSPLAN Ingenieurgesellschaft Geotechnik und Bergbau mbH 
Arnstaedter Strasse 28, 99096 Erfurt, Germany 

 

Phone: + 49 361 3810 220  Fax: +49 361 3810 402 e-mail: mining@ercosplan.com web: http://www.ercosplan.com 
 

O:\@Projekte EGB aktuell\EGB_10-012_MagIndustries_Makola_DeskStudy\03proj_AUSLIEFERUNG\NI43-101\Final_Report\2010_08_MagIndustries_Makola-rev-01.docx 

16.4.2 Conventional Underground Mining  

A conventional underground mine needs the sinking of 1 or 2 shafts down to the deposit and 
the development in the underground of drifts to start mining of the carnallitite ore. Conventional 
underground salt mining uses the room and pillar method, specifically adapted to each de-
posit. In this mining method rooms are mined out workings, which are separated from each 
other by pillars (part of the rock that is not mined) which support the roof and the overburden 
of the ore horizon. The ratio between rooms (material mined) and pillars (material left in the 
underground) is dependent on the material being mined, the depth of mining and the rock me-
chanical properties and hydrogeology of the overburden. Typical recovery ratios for conven-
tional mined carnallitite are between 20 to 40% area recovery. The high grade carnallitite in the 
deposit and the large depth of the deeper carnallitite layers in the SW part of the Makola Li-
cense area requires relative large pillars compared to the room size and the recovery would be 
in the lower part of the range for such configuration. However, for the NE part of the license 
area, near to the former Holle mine, high grade carnallitite layers occur at relatively shallow 
depth and here the recovery of a conventional mining operation would be relatively high.  

In salt mining special care must always be taken that with the dimensioning of room and pillars 
to guarantee that the rocks protecting the underground mine from groundwater bearing strata 
are not damaged. To prevent a flooding accident as for the conventional Holle Sylvite mine, 
(e.g. FEUGA ET AL., 2005  /8/), special care has to be taken during further investigations to 
identify potential subrosion structures. During operation, an intensive underground pre-mining 
exploration drilling program is necessary to prevent mining into a subrosion structure as hap-
pened in the Holle mine (FEUGA ET AL., 2005  /8). With such precautions there is no reason not 
to investigate conventional mining of Carnallite as an option.  

16.4.3 Underground Solution Mining  

An underground solution mining operation is only feasible if the ore material can be dissolved 
in a solvent. For salt rocks water or weak brine is an excellent solvent. For underground solu-
tion mining for a single cavern, 1 or 2 drill holes are sunk down to the deposit and the solvent is 
injected, dissolves the ore and extracted. In this way a cavern develops in the underground. 
Depending on the deposit mineralogy between 6 to 12 operating caverns are required for an 
annual production of 100,000 tpy. The concentrated brine is processed in the processing plant. 
To keep the surface stable cavern size and cavern spacing is limited, depending on the rock 
mechanical properties of material being mined and its overburden as well as on the depth of 
the operation. Typical area recovery ratios for underground solution mining operations are be-
tween 20 to 40%, depending on the thickness of the layer mined and the depth of the layers. 
With underground solution mining flooding can result in the loss of one or a few caverns, but 
overall the operation can continue.  

Solution mining is probably the best option to mine the thicker deep carnallitite layers in the 
SW part of the Makola License area (e.g. near Mengo) and might also be applicable for some 
of the shallow thick carnallitite layers.

16.4.4 Mining Method Summary 

For the carnallitite (and to lesser extent the sylvinite) ore present within the Makola License 
area, both conventional and solution mining are technical feasible options, which should be 
evaluated further together with the processing concept for the ore or the brine.  

Solution Mining is the preferred mining option where carnallitite layers occur with (i) high to 
mediocre grade, (ii) large thickness, and is (iii) independent of depth.  

Conventional Mining is the preferred mining option where carnallitite layers occur with (i) high 
grade at (ii) relatively shallow depth, and (iii) relatively independent of the thickness of the car-
nallitite layer.  

16.5 Mineral Resource Classification 

It is the opinion of the authors that taking into account all of the factors presented herein, the 
potash layers described before in chapter  8 can be economically extracted either by solution 
mining or conventional mining. Subject to further geological, geophysical, rock mechanical, 
and engineering studies these potash layers constitute a "Mineral Resource" as defined by 
National Instrument 43-101 and CIM (2005) /1/.The mineral resources are classified as inferred 
resources only.  

lnferred Mineral Resource 

For "Inferred Mineral Resources" NI 43-101 uses the definition of CIM (2005) /1/: 

An "Inferred Mineral Resource" is that part of a Mineral Resource for which quantity and 
grade or quality can be estimated on the basis of geological evidence and limited sam-
pling and reasonably assumed, but not verified, geological and grade continuity. The 
estimate is based on limited information and sampling gathered through appropriate 
techniques from locations such as outcrops, trenches, pits, workings and drill holes. 

In the opinion of the authors the inferred class of mineral resources extends to the resource in 
carnallitite Horizons 1 to 7, within 3km radius of a historical drill hole with assay data , or 1.5km 
for single potash layers and sylvinite ore. Based on this definition inferred resources for the drill 
holes K01,K09, K10, K11, K12, K14, K15, K16, K17, K23, K24, K25, K26, K27, K28, K29, K30, 
K31, K33, K34, K35, K36, K37, K40, K41, K42, K43, K45, K46, K48, K54, K56, K59B, K71, K75, 
K76, K80, K83, K84, K85, K 86, K88, KOU08, KOU09, Mengo12, Mengo13, Mengo15 have been 
defined.

resulting inferred carnallitite ore resources are listed in Table 7.

It has been assumed that within the extrapolated drill hole area (polygon) the grade and thick-
ness of the potash horizons are uniform.  

There are large changes in the number of drill holes used for resource estimation from a hori-
zon or potash layer for geological reasons (horizons/layers not present) but mainly because 
the exploration that resulted in opening of the Holle mine, concentrated on the Sylvite present 
in horizon 3 and horizon 4 and did not intensively sample and assay the non Sylvite potash 
layers below these horizons and, especially during later stages of the exploration, stopped drill-
ing after having reached horizon 4.  

The total inferred sylvinite resources from the different horizons and layers are summarised in 
Table 27 and amount to 70 million tonnes of sylvinite ore with an average K2O grade of almost 
21%. The total inferred carnallitite resources from the different horizons and layers are summa-
rised in Table 28 and amount to almost 9200 million tonnes of carnallitite ore with an average 
K2O grade of 12%.

Indicated and Measured Mineral Resource 

For "Indicated Mineral Resources" and "Measured Mineral Resources", NI 43-101 uses the 
definition of CIM (2005  /2/): 

An "Indicated Mineral Resource" is that part of a Mineral Resource for which quantity, 
grade or quality, densities, shape and physical characteristics can be estimated with a 
level of confidence sufficient to allow the appropriate application of technical and eco-
nomic parameters, to support mine planning and evaluation of the economic viability of 
the deposit. The estimate is based on detailed and reliable exploration and testing in-
formation gathered through appropriate techniques from locations such as outcrops, 



 
 
 
 
Technical  Scoping  Study, Makola Exploration License, Kouilou Region, Republic of Congo 
EGB 10-012 
 
 

 
Page 86 of 97 

 

ERCOSPLAN Ingenieurgesellschaft Geotechnik und Bergbau mbH 
Arnstaedter Strasse 28, 99096 Erfurt, Germany 

 

Phone: + 49 361 3810 220  Fax: +49 361 3810 402 e-mail: mining@ercosplan.com web: http://www.ercosplan.com 
 

O:\@Projekte EGB aktuell\EGB_10-012_MagIndustries_Makola_DeskStudy\03proj_AUSLIEFERUNG\NI43-101\Final_Report\2010_08_MagIndustries_Makola-rev-01.docx 

trenches, pits, workings and drill holes that are spaced closely enough for geological 
and grade continuity to be reasonably assumed. 

A "Measured Mineral Resource" is that part of a Mineral Resource for which quantity, 
grade or quality, densities, shape, and physical characteristics are so well established 
that they can be estimated with confidence sufficient to allow the appropriate applica-
tion of technical and economic parameters, to support production planning and evalua-
tion of the economic viability of the deposit. The estimate is based on detailed and reli-
able exploration, sampling and testing information gathered through appropriate tech-
niques from locations such as outcrops, trenches, pits, workings and drill holes that are 
spaced closely enough to confirm both geological and grade continuity. 

In the opinion of the authors the classes of "Measured" and "Indicated" Mineral resources re-
quire a higher level of confidence than the broader description "Inferred Mineral Resource". In 
the case of a bedded sedimentary deposit that is not revealed in outcrop and only testable 
through drilling or mine workings, one key determinant for the elevation of resources to the 
measured or indicated category is the existence of material from the deposit to verify assaying 
and test work data. Some carnallitite core material has been studied from the Mengo area (e.g. 
ERCOSPLAN 2009 /11/), but this provides just a general confirmation of the presence of carnal-
litite and the properties of the material from that area. It cannot be used to verify the historical 
assaying data as no historical assaying data exist for the only historic drill hole K03 that can be 
compared with the recent data. In the opinion of the authors the amount of historical data 
available in some parts of the license area would allow the classification of "indicated and 
measured mineral resources" if verification of these data would be possible. Without this verifi-
cation it is the opinion of the authors that no "measured or indicated mineral resources" can be 
classified for the license area.  

16.6 Mineral Reserve Classification 

The definition of "Mineral Reserve" states, that to transform "Indicated or Measured" Mineral 
Resources to a "Mineral Reserve" the economic mineability of these Resources has to be 
demonstrated by at least a pre-feasibility study. Since no recent pre-feasibility study has been 
completed to justify economic viability, none of the resources can be classified as reserve at 
this time.

No other data and information is considered necessary at this time

18 Interpretation and Conclusions 

Potash mineralisation in the form of carnallitite underlies substantially all of the Makola License 
area in up to 12 potentially mineable layers. Locally, assumed to be tectonically controlled, 
some of the carnallitite layers have been transformed to a sylvite, resulting in a high grade syl-
vinite mineralisation. Based on a coarse geologic model the location of a number of fault zones 
have been identified, which might pose problems during mining of the potash ore and these 
areas have been excluded from the resource area. Also the mine workings of the flooded Holle 
mine have been excluded from the resource area.  

All drill holes in the immediate proximity of the license area that have penetrated the evaporite 
sequence have encountered one or several potash bearing beds, but not in all drill holes have 
all potash layers been samples and assayed. A differentiation has been made between potash 
horizons that can be correlated in adequate thickness over large parts of the license area and 
potash layers that occur in adequate thickness in single drill holes or restricted parts of the li-
cense area. In total 119 carnallitite potash layers/horizons and 14 sylvinite potash lay-
ers/horizons, not located in exclusion zones have been sampled and have reported assays as 
basis for the resource estimation.  

The following is the averaged thickness and K2O content of the different mineralized Horizons:  

• Horizon 1 carnallitite: 4 holes with 19.2m thickness at 10.5% K2O 

• Horizon 2 carnallitite: 6 holes with 8.4m thickness at 9.1% K2O 

• Horizon 2 sylvinite: 2 holes with 3.3m thickness at 15.7% K2O 

• Layer 9 of cycle IX carnallitite: 1 hole with 4.4m thickness at 11.8% K2O 

• Layers 6,7 and 8 of cycle IX carnallitite:8 holes with 7.9m thickness at 8.6% K2O 

• Layers 6,7 and 8 of cycle IX sylvinite:1 hole with 7.4m thickness at 13.6% K2O 

• Horizon 3 carnallitite: 28 holes with 8.7m thickness at 15.8% K2O 

• Horizon 3 sylvinite: 9 holes with 3.0m thickness at 39.1% K2O 

• Layer 4 of cycle VIII carnallitite: 1 hole with 5.8m thickness at 11.2% K2O 

• Horizon 4 carnallitite: 34 holes with 11.6m thickness at 12.6% K2O 

• Horizon 4 sylvinite: 2 holes with 3.1m thickness at 35.5% K2O 

• Layer 1 of cycle VIII carnallitite: 2 holes with 4.8m thickness at 14.3% K2O 

• Horizon 5 carnallitite: 8 holes with 4.5m thickness at 9.2% K2O 

• Layers 3 and 4 of cycle VII carnallitite:7 holes with 6.0m thickness at 9.6% K2O 

• Layer 2 of cycle VII carnallitite: 5 holes with 5.9m thickness at 11.5% K2O 

• Layers of cycle VI carnallitite: 6 holes with 9.0m thickness at 8.7% K2O 

• Layers of cycle V carnallitite: 1 hole with 13.8m thickness at 10.0% K2O 

• Horizon 6 carnallitite: 2 holes with 4.8m thickness at 6.7% K2O 

• Layers of cycle III carnallitite: 2 holes with 4.5m thickness at 13.7% K2O 

• Horizon 7 carnallitite: 5 holes with 9.7m thickness at 10.9% K2O 



 
 
 
 
Technical  Scoping  Study, Makola Exploration License, Kouilou Region, Republic of Congo 
EGB 10-012

ERCOSPLAN Ingenieurgesellschaft Geotechnik und Bergbau mbH 
Arnstaedter Strasse 28, 99096 Erfurt, Germany

O:\@Projekte EGB aktuell\EGB_10-012_MagIndustries_Makola_DeskStudy\03proj_AUSLIEFERUNG\NI43-101\Final_Report\2010_08_MagIndustries_Makola-rev-01.docx 

Inferred resources have been estimated based on the distance from a cored and assayed drill 
hole using best available practice. Where drill holes have overlapping confidence intervals, a 
polygon around the holes is constructed. The distance from an existing cored and assayed 
hole for the inclusion of resource in the inferred category is as follows:  

• 3000m radius for carnallitite Horizons that could be correlated over most of the li-
cence areas  

• 1500m radius for sylvinite Horizons or carnallitite/sylvinite layers that could not be 
correlated between several drill holes, or were only identified in restricted areas of 
the licence area. 

The inferred resources have been estimated using the confidence area around a drill hole, 
thickness and grade of the potash layer/horizon, a loss factor of 35% for geological and tech-
nical exclusion zones for carnallitite based on the detail exploration near Mengo and based on 
a study of the plans of the Holle Mine of 50% for sylvinite. Not included in the estimate are min-
ing losses, dilution and plant recovery.  

Based on these assumptions the estimated inferred resources over the whole area amount to  

~9200 million tonnes of carnallitite ore at 12% K2O, 

~ 70 million tonnes of sylvinite or at 21% K2O. 

No measured and indicated resources have been estimated, because an independent verifica-
tion of the assaying results was not possible. 

Large areas of the license area have not been drilled and no estimation can be made for the 
potash present in these areas. The historical seismic data could not be used in the geological 
model and faults and potential subrosion structures, have been interpreted based only on 
analysis of aerial and satellite images. Further study consisting of (i) detailed field work, (ii) 
seismic and eventually also (iii) drilling will be required to further the full extent of these struc-
tures in the whole or parts of the license area. The consequence of this work can be that larger 
areas have to be excluded as presently estimated and the outcome might be a decrease in 
mineral resource.  

In the opinion of the authors the Makola Exploration License area contains sufficient inferred 
resources to justify further expenditures of funds to confirm and expand the resource and to 
develop indicated and measured resources and reserves in compliance with NI 43-101. Explo-
ration, engineering, geotechnical, and economic studies are required to develop indicated and 
measured resources and to estimate reserves.  

It is also the opinion of the authors that it is reasonable to conclude that potash mineralization 
as identified in the drill cores may extend to the remainder of the license area and that further 
drilling to confirm the presence of potash is warranted. At present the resources come from 
only about 25% of the license area and in this area assaying has been done usually on less 
than 50% of potential mineable potash horizons.   

Additional work is required to further investigate three areas of risk:  

(a) Quality Risk: The risk is uncertainty regarding the quality of the potash mineraliza-
tion in the license area due to (i) the non possibility to verify the historical assaying 
data and (ii) large areas of the license area have not yet been drilled. This risk can 
be mitigated by (i) drilling of probably 2 confirmation drill holes close (~ 200m) to 
historical drill holes with assay data, that will be completely cored, sampled and as-
sayed to present day QA/QC procedures and compare the results with the historical 



 
 
 
 
Technical  Scoping  Study, Makola Exploration License, Kouilou Region, Republic of Congo 
EGB 10-012 
 
 

 
Page 89 of 97 

 

ERCOSPLAN Ingenieurgesellschaft Geotechnik und Bergbau mbH 
Arnstaedter Strasse 28, 99096 Erfurt, Germany 

 

Phone: + 49 361 3810 220  Fax: +49 361 3810 402 e-mail: mining@ercosplan.com web: http://www.ercosplan.com 
 

O:\@Projekte EGB aktuell\EGB_10-012_MagIndustries_Makola_DeskStudy\03proj_AUSLIEFERUNG\NI43-101\Final_Report\2010_08_MagIndustries_Makola-rev-01.docx 

assaying data and (ii) the drilling of up to 7 or 8 surface drill holes to cover a large 
part of the 75% of the license area not covered by drill holes. 

(b) Continuity Risk: This risk is the uncertainty concerning the continuity of potash mi-
neralisation between the present drill holes. This risk can be mitigated by re-
interpretation of existing "2D" seismic trade lines and/or acquisition of new 2D seis-
mic lines as to confirm the continuity of the potash mineralization.  

(c) Mining and Processing Risks: The risk is related to the quality risk, as without quality 
data about the deposit no meaningful processing and mining concept can be made. 
This risk can be mitigated when quality controlled data about the deposit are availa-
ble by the preparation of a conceptual mining and processing study aimed at identi-
fying basic mining and processing concepts and run preliminary scenarios with dif-
ferent deposit depth, deposit thickness and energy costs to identifying potential 
economic options for a project in certain parts of the license area. The preliminary 
level of this engineering evaluation would provide the basis for the decision in un-
dertaking a pre-feasibility study. 

19 Recommendations 

The authors of the report recommend a phased development of the project consisting of 

19.1 Confirmation Phase  

This phase consists of the drilling of 4 small diameter confirmation drill holes near historical drill 
holes with assays to confirm the reliability of the historical assaying data and to obtain reliable 
data for a scoping study that identifies potential mining and processing methods. It is recom-
mended to have confirmation holes:  

Near exploration hole K30 to be able to investigate the option of solution mining large 
thickness, mediocre to high grade carnallitite layers and processing of the solution 
mined brine. This area could provide additional resources for MAGMINERALS planned 
Mengo Project;  

Near exploration hole K17 to be able to investigate the option of conventional mining of 
restricted thickness but high grade carnallitite layers at relatively shallow depths (e.g. 
Horizon 7 carnallitite at 740m depth) and processing of the conventional mined carnalli-
tite ore;  

Near exploration hole K40 to be able to investigate the option of conventional mining of 
high grade sylvinite ore combined with conventional mining of restricted thickness but 
high grade carnallitite layers at relatively shallow depths (e.g. Horizon 7 carnallitite at 
660m depth) and combined or separate processing of the conventional mined carnalli-
tite and sylvinite ore. This option might also investigate, whether the brine from the 
flooded Holle mine can be used for potash production or not; 



 
 
 
 
Technical  Scoping  Study, Makola Exploration License, Kouilou Region, Republic of Congo 
EGB 10-012 
 
 

 
Page 90 of 97 

 

ERCOSPLAN Ingenieurgesellschaft Geotechnik und Bergbau mbH 
Arnstaedter Strasse 28, 99096 Erfurt, Germany 

 

Phone: + 49 361 3810 220  Fax: +49 361 3810 402 e-mail: mining@ercosplan.com web: http://www.ercosplan.com 
 

O:\@Projekte EGB aktuell\EGB_10-012_MagIndustries_Makola_DeskStudy\03proj_AUSLIEFERUNG\NI43-101\Final_Report\2010_08_MagIndustries_Makola-rev-01.docx 

Near exploration hole K25 to be able to investigate the option of conventional mining of 
low grade sylvinite ore combined with conventional mining of restricted thickness but 
high grade carnallitite layers at relatively shallow depths (e.g. Horizon 7 carnallitite at 
790m depth) and combined or separate processing of the conventional mined carnalli-
tite and sylvinite ore.  

The costs for drilling of the 4 small diameter confirmation drill holes and the geological supervi-
sion of the drilling, core description, sampling and assaying organized by the authors or a reg-
istered Professional Geologist with experience in potash are estimated to amount to about 2.3 
million EURO.  

19.2 The Scoping Phase 

Conditional upon favorable outcome of Phase 1, continue with a scoping (order of magnitude) 
study of the 4 mining and processing alternatives discussed for the confirmation phase to iden-
tify the most promising concept and to provide a basis to estimate in some detail the permit-
ting, mining, processing, transport, marketing and economic basis for the project. The esti-
mated costs for this study amount to about 0.25 million EURO.  

In parallel to the Scoping Study of the 4 mining and processing alternatives, further geological 
studies should be done to refine the geological model. These studies consist of aerial and sa-
tellite image lineation interpretation to refine the MDPA (1989) interpretation, combined with 
ground check of the identified structures and re-evaluation of the available historical 2D seismic 
data. This should provide an updated geological model that can be used to identify further 
areas within the license area, where the geological circumstances might be comparable to the 
alternatives studied. The outcome of this study should for each studied alternative provide up 
to 2 alternative areas in the license area, where the geological circumstances are comparable 
to that of the alternatives studied. The estimated costs for this study amount to about 
50,000 EURO.  

19.3 Selection and Pre-Feasibility Phase 

Conditional upon favorable outcome from Phase 2, continue studies to confirm and expand the 
resource base and complete a preliminary feasibility study that includes:  

(1) drilling exploration with 1 or 2 surface holes in each of the identified 2 to 4 areas with 
geological circumstances suitable for the alternatives with the best outcome of the 
scoping study. After a first round of small diameter exploration holes in each area, 2 
more large diameter holes (4" core) will be drilled to the deposit. The goal of the drill-
ing of these 4 to 6 holes is  

• to confirm and refine the geological model 

• provide adequate sized samples for rock mechanical and other test work 

• extend the area of indicated/measured resource within the license area

The costs for drilling exploration with the geological supervision of the drilling, core 
description, sampling and assaying organized by the authors or a registered Profes-
sional Geologist with experience in potash are estimated to amount to about 4.0 mil-
lion EURO. 

(2) complete geotechnical and other test work on the core material 

(3) complete mine designs, process designs and marketing studies 

(4) complete a pre-feasibility study to support definition of measured mineral resources 
and reserves  

The total costs for test works, design works and further studies with completion of a pre-
feasibility study are estimated to be in the order of 1.5 million EURO 

19.4 Detail Exploration and Feasibility Phase 

Conditional upon favorable outcome from Phase 3, proceed to a definitive feasibility study. This 
requires concentration of exploration on a single area with probably drilling of further holes and 
detail engineering to support the final mining and processing concept.  

19.5 Estimated Project Costs and Time Frame 

Phase 1 is estimated to cost about 2.3 million EURO and could be completed in 10 months 

Phase 2 is estimated to cost about 0.3 million EURO and could be completed in 6 months 

Phase 3 is estimated to cost about 4 million EURO for exploration, and 1.5 million EURO for 
further studies and could be completed in 18 months 

Phase 4 costs and time frame cannot be reasonably estimated yet. 

  



 
 
 
 
Technical  Scoping  Study, Makola Exploration License, Kouilou Region, Republic of Congo
